A 16-year-old sustains a first-time lateral patellar dislocation that has spontaneously reduced. MRI shows an inferomedial patellar facet and anterolateral femoral condyle bone bruise and an MPFL tear, with no displaced osteochondral fragment and a TT-TG distance of 12 mm. What is the most appropriate initial management?

- A. Nonoperative rehabilitation with bracing and quadriceps strengthening [CORRECT]
- B. Acute MPFL reconstruction with tibial tubercle osteotomy
- C. Lateral retinacular release alone
- D. Immediate patellectomy
Explanation: A first-time dislocation without a loose osteochondral body and with a normal TT-TG (well under the ~20 mm threshold) is managed nonoperatively with bracing and rehabilitation. Tibial tubercle osteotomy is reserved for an elevated TT-TG, so adding it here would be over-treatment given the normal alignment measurement.

#### Advanced: caq-8 · Posterolateral corner — combined injury / graft failure

A 27-year-old has an MRI-confirmed posterolateral corner injury with fibular collateral ligament, popliteus, and popliteofibular ligament disruption after a varus hyperextension blow. The surgeon plans cruciate reconstruction. Why is recognizing and addressing this posterolateral corner injury before isolated cruciate surgery most important?

- A. Unrecognized posterolateral corner injury is a leading cause of cruciate graft failure from residual rotational and varus instability [CORRECT]
- B. Posterolateral corner injuries reliably heal nonoperatively, so cruciate surgery can simply be delayed
- C. The posterolateral corner has no effect on cruciate graft loading once the cruciate is reconstructed
- D. Posterolateral corner injuries occur in isolation, so they rarely change the surgical plan
Explanation: The posterolateral corner is the primary restraint to varus and external-rotation, and a missed combined injury leaves residual rotational/varus laxity that overloads and stretches out a cruciate graft, a recognized cause of reconstruction failure, so it must be addressed concurrently. The distractors are wrong because high-grade posterolateral corner injuries usually require repair or reconstruction rather than healing alone, and they very commonly accompany (rather than spare) cruciate injury.

#### Advanced: cruciates-1 · Cruciate ligaments (ACL/PCL)

A 24-year-old presents after a pivoting injury. Sagittal MRI shows the ACL is poorly visualized. To use the posterior cruciate ligament (PCL) line sign as a secondary indicator of ACL tear, you draw a line along the straight distal segment of the PCL and extend it proximally and anteriorly. In an intact knee, where should this line intersect?

- A. It should intersect the femur, crossing the distal femoral metaphysis/medullary canal [CORRECT]
- B. It should intersect the tibial plateau just anterior to the tibial tubercle
- C. It should fail to intersect the femur and instead pass anterior to it
- D. It should intersect the patella at its lower pole
Explanation: Normally the extended PCL line intersects the femoral medullary cavity; with a torn ACL the PCL becomes more buckled/redundant (more sharply curved) from anterior tibial translation, so the line falls short and fails to intersect the femur (a positive sign). It is a secondary sign best combined with primary findings.

#### Advanced: cruciates-2 · Cruciate ligaments (ACL/PCL)

On MRI of a knee with an acute ACL tear, you measure anterior tibial translation by referencing the posterior margins of the lateral femoral condyle and lateral tibial plateau on a sagittal image. Which threshold of anterior tibial subluxation is most specific for a complete ACL tear?

- A. ≥ 1 mm
- B. ≥ 3 mm
- C. ≥ 5 mm [CORRECT]
- D. ≥ 10 mm
Explanation: Anterior translation of the lateral tibial plateau of ≥ 5 mm relative to the lateral femoral condyle is highly specific (though not very sensitive) for a complete ACL tear; smaller degrees overlap with normal laxity.

#### Advanced: cruciates-3 · Cruciate ligaments (ACL/PCL)

A 52-year-old with chronic, atraumatic knee pain has an ACL that is diffusely thickened and ill-defined with intermediate-to-high T2 signal, yet the individual fibers remain continuous and parallel, producing a 'celery-stalk' appearance. There is often an associated finding in the adjacent bone. Which is the single best diagnosis and its characteristic associated feature?

- A. Acute complete ACL tear with a Segond fracture of the lateral tibia
- B. ACL mucoid degeneration, frequently associated with subchondral/intraosseous ganglion cysts at the tibial or femoral attachment [CORRECT]
- C. ACL graft impingement with a cyclops lesion in Hoffa's fat pad
- D. ACL ganglion arising from the PCL with a deep lateral femoral notch sign
Explanation: Mucoid degeneration shows a thickened ACL with continuous, intact fibers ('celery stalk' / increased signal but preserved striations) and is commonly accompanied by intraosseous ganglion cysts at the ligament insertions — distinguishing it from a tear, where fibers are discontinuous.

#### Advanced: cruciates-4 · Cruciate ligaments (ACL/PCL)

Reviewing the radiograph and MRI of an acute knee injury, you identify a deep lateral femoral notch (terminal/condylopatellar sulcus). Beyond simply suggesting an ACL tear, an abnormally deep notch is considered a useful marker. Which depth threshold is the accepted cutoff that strongly suggests an associated ACL injury?

- A. Greater than 0.5 mm
- B. Greater than 1.5 mm [CORRECT]
- C. Greater than 4 mm
- D. Greater than 8 mm
Explanation: A lateral femoral (condylopatellar) sulcus depth greater than 1.5 mm is the classic threshold strongly associated with ACL disruption, reflecting an osteochondral impaction injury as the anteriorly subluxed tibia engages the lateral femoral condyle.

#### Advanced: menisci-1 · Menisci

On sagittal MR images with 4-5 mm slice thickness, which finding most reliably distinguishes a discoid lateral meniscus from a normal lateral meniscus?

- A. A minimum coronal body width of 6-8 mm on the central coronal image
- B. The 'bow-tie' (continuous body) appearance on 3 or more contiguous sagittal slices [CORRECT]
- C. Absence of the normal meniscofemoral ligament on the Wrisberg side
- D. Increased intrameniscal grade 2 signal in the posterior horn
Explanation: A normal meniscus shows the contiguous 'bow-tie' body on at most 2 consecutive 4-5 mm sagittal slices; 3 or more contiguous bow-tie segments (or a central coronal width >13-15 mm) indicates a discoid meniscus.

#### Advanced: menisci-2 · Menisci

A 58-year-old has acute medial knee pain. Coronal MRI shows the posterior root signal disrupted with the meniscal body displaced 4 mm beyond the tibial margin. Regarding the 3 mm extrusion threshold for a medial meniscal posterior root tear, which statement is most accurate?

- A. The 3 mm threshold is a useful high-specificity clue but relatively insensitive, so extrusion <3 mm does not exclude a root tear [CORRECT]
- B. The 3 mm threshold is both highly sensitive and highly specific for root tears
- C. Extrusion is best and most reproducibly measured on sagittal rather than coronal images
- D. Meniscal extrusion of this degree is a normal finding and requires no root evaluation
Explanation: Coronal medial meniscal extrusion >=3 mm is a significant clue in many root-tear studies, but it is only modestly sensitive and should not be treated as diagnostic by itself. Sub-3 mm extrusion does not exclude a root tear; the 'ghost' (absent/blunted root) sign should also be sought.

#### Advanced: menisci-3 · Menisci

During ACL reconstruction planning, you are asked about a suspected ramp lesion. Which statement is most accurate regarding its MRI and arthroscopic evaluation?

- A. It is a radial tear of the posterior horn lateral meniscus best seen on coronal images
- B. Standard anterior-portal arthroscopy reliably visualizes it, so MRI adds little
- C. Fluid signal between the posterior horn medial meniscus and the joint capsule on fluid-sensitive sequences is a key sign, and the lesion is frequently missed without posteromedial probing [CORRECT]
- D. It is defined as extrusion of the medial meniscus body greater than 5 mm
Explanation: A ramp lesion is a tear at the posteromedial meniscocapsular junction; high T2 fluid signal/edema posterior to the posterior horn raises suspicion, and a substantial proportion are missed on standard anterior-portal viewing without a posteromedial (trans-notch) probing approach.

#### Advanced: menisci-4 · Menisci

On sagittal MRI of a knee with an ACL tear, you see vertical signal at the junction of the posterior horn lateral meniscus and an obliquely coursing structure passing toward the PCL. Which feature best favors a true posterior horn tear over a meniscofemoral (Wrisberg) ligament pseudotear?

- A. The vertical signal can be traced contiguously into the PCL through the intercondylar notch
- B. The apparent meniscal cleft persists 4 or more sagittal images lateral to the PCL [CORRECT]
- C. The structure lies posterior to the PCL on the Wrisberg side
- D. The signal is seen on only a single sagittal image near the midline
Explanation: A Wrisberg/Humphrey pseudotear is created where the meniscofemoral ligament inserts on the posterior horn; an apparent cleft extending >=4 sagittal images lateral to the PCL exceeds the ligament's reach and should be treated as a true tear, especially with an ACL injury.

#### Advanced: cartilage-1 · Cartilage & osteochondral

A 16-year-old soccer player has a juvenile osteochondritis dissecans (OCD) lesion at the classic lateral aspect of the medial femoral condyle. On fluid-sensitive fat-suppressed sequences, which finding is MOST specific for an UNSTABLE fragment?

- A. A thin, uninterrupted line of high T2 signal isointense to articular cartilage along the fragment interface
- B. A high-T2-signal rim completely surrounding the fragment that follows fluid signal intensity and undercuts it [CORRECT]
- C. Mild bone-marrow edema in the parent bone deep to the lesion
- D. An en-face fragment diameter measuring 6 mm with intact overlying cartilage
Explanation: A fluid-signal-intensity rim that completely encircles and undercuts the fragment indicates synovial fluid dissecting the interface and is the most reliable De Smet sign of instability; a granulation-tissue interface line is only as bright as cartilage, not fluid. Secondary signs include subchondral cysts >5 mm and a focal cartilage defect.

#### Advanced: cartilage-2 · Cartilage & osteochondral

When applying the De Smet MRI criteria for OCD fragment instability, the SPECIFICITY of a T2-bright line at the fragment-parent interface improves substantially when which additional qualifier is present?

- A. The patient is skeletally immature with open physes
- B. The high-signal line is matched by a breach (defect) in the overlying articular cartilage on fluid-sensitive images [CORRECT]
- C. The lesion is located in the patellofemoral compartment
- D. The fragment measures less than 5 mm in greatest dimension
Explanation: In skeletally immature patients a T2-bright interface line frequently reflects vascular granulation tissue rather than fluid, lowering specificity; instability is far more reliable when that line is accompanied by a discontinuity in the overlying cartilage, confirming communication with the joint.

#### Advanced: cartilage-4 · Cartilage & osteochondral

A surgeon asks you to distinguish chondral DELAMINATION from a simple partial-thickness fissure on knee MRI. Which finding is MOST characteristic of delamination?

- A. A linear high-signal cleft extending perpendicularly from the articular surface into but not through the cartilage
- B. A fluid-signal line at the cartilage-bone (osteochondral) junction with the overlying cartilage surface still intact [CORRECT]
- C. Focal full-thickness cartilage loss exposing subchondral bone (ICRS grade 4)
- D. Diffuse intrachondral T2 hyperintensity without a discrete defect
Explanation: Delamination is a shear separation of cartilage from subchondral bone at the deep tidemark/calcified-cartilage zone, seen as a fluid-signal line paralleling the bone with a deceptively intact articular surface; it can be missed at arthroscopic probing of the surface.

#### Advanced: patellofemoral-1 · Patellofemoral & extensor

A sports-medicine fellow measures a tibial tubercle–trochlear groove (TT-TG) distance of 22 mm on an axial MRI in a patient with recurrent lateral patellar instability. Above which commonly cited threshold does the TT-TG distance become a recognized risk factor for patellar instability and an indication potentially favoring tibial tubercle medialization?

- A. 8 mm
- B. 12 mm
- C. 20 mm [CORRECT]
- D. 30 mm
Explanation: A TT-TG distance >=20 mm is the classic abnormal threshold associated with patellar instability, but it is a risk marker rather than a standalone surgical rule. MRI values measured on cartilaginous landmarks tend to run a few millimeters lower than CT-based bony measurements, so landmarks and clinical context matter.

#### Advanced: patellofemoral-2 · Patellofemoral & extensor

Which patella-alta index is least confounded by patellar tendon length variability because it uses the patellar articular surface relative to the tibial plateau rather than the tendon?

- A. Insall-Salvati ratio
- B. Caton-Deschamps index [CORRECT]
- C. Blackburne-Peel index measured to the patellar tendon insertion
- D. Modified Insall-Salvati ratio measured to the inferior pole of the patella
Explanation: The Caton-Deschamps index (distance from the inferior articular margin of the patella to the anterosuperior tibial plateau divided by patellar articular surface length; alta if >1.2) avoids errors from a variable or thickened patellar tendon that confound the Insall-Salvati ratio. Note it is a RADIOGRAPHIC index — measure it on a lateral radiograph, since single-slice MRI inflates it by up to ~9%. It is not the most cross-modality-reliable index overall (Insall-Salvati is more reproducible across modalities); it is simply the one least affected by tendon variability.

#### Advanced: patellofemoral-3 · Patellofemoral & extensor

On a true lateral radiograph or sagittal MRI of a knee with trochlear dysplasia, the 'crossing sign' refers to which specific finding indicating a flat or shallow trochlea?

- A. The line of the trochlear floor crosses the anterior cortex of the lateral femoral condyle [CORRECT]
- B. The patellar tendon crosses the physeal scar at the level of the tibial tubercle
- C. The medial and lateral trochlear facet lines cross at the superior pole of the patella
- D. The supratrochlear spur crosses the Blumensaat line
Explanation: The crossing sign is present when the line representing the floor of the trochlear groove crosses the anterior border of the lateral femoral condyle, meaning the groove becomes flat (flush with the condyle) at that point—a hallmark of trochlear dysplasia. A supratrochlear spur and double contour are additional Dejour signs of higher-grade dysplasia.

#### Advanced: patellofemoral-4 · Patellofemoral & extensor

A 16-year-old presents after a transient lateral patellar dislocation. Beyond the classic medial patellar and lateral femoral condyle bone bruise pattern and MPFL injury, which additional finding is a well-described NORMAL VARIANT of the patella that should NOT be mistaken for an osteochondral injury or osteomyelitis?

- A. Dorsal defect of the patella in the superolateral quadrant [CORRECT]
- B. Bipartite patella fragment in the inferomedial pole
- C. Osteochondral fracture of the median ridge
- D. Avulsion at the adductor tubercle MPFL origin
Explanation: The dorsal defect of the patella is a benign subchondral lucency in the superolateral quadrant of the patella with intact overlying cartilage on MRI; recognizing the intact cartilage and typical location prevents misdiagnosis as an osteochondral lesion or infection. In lateral patellar dislocation the MPFL can tear at its patellar attachment, its femoral (adductor tubercle) attachment, or both — the tear site is variable.

#### Advanced: corners-1 · Collaterals & posterolateral corner

On a coronal MRI of an acutely injured knee, you identify a curvilinear bone fragment avulsed from the fibula and displaced superomedially, accompanied by edema in the popliteus and fibular collateral ligament. Which anatomic structure is most specifically responsible for this 'arcuate sign' avulsion fragment?

- A. The arcuate ligament / conjoined insertion of the posterolateral corner at the fibular styloid [CORRECT]
- B. The proximal tibiofibular ligament at the fibular head
- C. The fibular insertion of the lateral head of gastrocnemius
- D. The fabellofibular ligament alone at the fibular apex
Explanation: The arcuate sign is a small avulsion at the fibular styloid, the conjoined attachment site of the posterolateral corner (arcuate ligament complex), which is why it signals a high rate of associated cruciate (especially PCL) injury and should never be dismissed as trivial.

#### Advanced: corners-2 · Collaterals & posterolateral corner

A fellow shows you a coronal proton-density image with ossification along the expected course of the proximal MCL, paralleling the medial femoral condyle, in a patient with a remote valgus injury and no acute edema. To distinguish a healed grade I-II MCL injury (Pellegrini-Stieda) from an acute deep MCL tear, which feature most reliably indicates the chronic, healed process?

- A. Curvilinear ossification at the femoral origin without surrounding marrow or soft-tissue edema [CORRECT]
- B. Fluid signal interposed between the deep MCL and the medial meniscus
- C. A wavy, lax superficial MCL with surrounding T2-hyperintense fluid
- D. High T2 signal tracking along the meniscofemoral and meniscotibial ligaments
Explanation: Pellegrini-Stieda represents post-traumatic ossification/calcification at the femoral attachment of the MCL; the absence of marrow and soft-tissue edema confirms a chronic healed lesion rather than acute injury, where edema and fluid would dominate.

#### Advanced: corners-3 · Collaterals & posterolateral corner

When grading a medial collateral ligament injury on MRI for a sports-medicine fellow, which finding is MOST consistent with a high-grade (grade III) complete tear rather than a grade II partial injury?

- A. Complete discontinuity of the superficial MCL fibers with fluid spanning the full ligament width and a wavy, retracted free edge [CORRECT]
- B. Edema superficial to an intact, thickened MCL with preserved continuous low-signal fibers
- C. Periligamentous edema with partial fiber thickening but no fluid-filled defect
- D. Increased T2 signal within the deep MCL with intact superficial fibers
Explanation: Grade III is defined by complete fiber discontinuity with a fluid-filled gap and often a wavy/retracted ligament, whereas grade I shows only periligamentous edema over intact fibers and grade II shows partial-thickness fiber disruption or thickening with preserved continuity.

#### Advanced: corners-4 · Collaterals & posterolateral corner

On axial and sagittal MRI at the level of the lateral knee, you trace a tendon to the fibular head, where it blends with the fibular (lateral) collateral ligament to form a 'conjoined tendon.' Recognizing this conjoined insertion is key because injury here implicates posterolateral instability. Which structure forms this conjoined insertion with the fibular collateral ligament?

- A. The biceps femoris tendon, whose long-head direct and anterior arms blend with the FCL at the fibular head [CORRECT]
- B. The iliotibial band, which inserts at Gerdy tubercle on the lateral tibia and does not conjoin with the FCL
- C. The popliteus tendon, which passes deep to the FCL to insert on the lateral femoral condyle
- D. The lateral patellar retinaculum, which extends to the fibular head
Explanation: The long head of the biceps femoris and the fibular (lateral) collateral ligament merge into a conjoined tendon at the fibular head; the IT band inserts separately at Gerdy tubercle and the popliteus inserts on the femur, so recognizing the conjoined insertion helps localize posterolateral corner injury.

#### Advanced: marrow-1 · Bone marrow & fractures

A 24-year-old presents after a non-contact pivoting injury. MRI shows ACL discontinuity. Regarding the classic pivot-shift contusion pattern, which paired location of marrow edema is MOST specific for this mechanism?

- A. Posterolateral tibial plateau and mid-lateral femoral condyle (near the terminal sulcus) [CORRECT]
- B. Anteromedial femoral condyle and anteromedial tibial plateau
- C. Posteromedial tibial plateau and medial femoral condyle
- D. Anterior tibia and anterior femur with patellar contusion
Explanation: The pivot-shift (a valgus load on the flexed knee with rotation and anterior translation) drives the posterolateral tibial plateau against the mid-portion of the lateral femoral condyle at the condylopatellar (terminal) sulcus, producing the kissing contusions highly associated with ACL tears. A reciprocal posteromedial tibial plateau bruise from the tibia snapping back is a secondary sign. (The exact rotation direction is described inconsistently — the injury source describes external tibial rotation, the clinical pivot-shift test internal — so 'valgus with rotation' is the safe phrasing.)

#### Advanced: marrow-2 · Bone marrow & fractures

A 67-year-old woman has acute medial knee pain without trauma. MRI shows a subchondral focus in the weight-bearing medial femoral condyle. Which finding BEST distinguishes a subchondral insufficiency fracture (SIFK/SONK) from primary osteonecrosis (AVN)?

- A. A subchondral T2-hypointense/low-signal line that is concave toward and roughly parallel to the subchondral plate, with adjacent diffuse marrow edema [CORRECT]
- B. A serpentine double-line sign enclosing a fat-signal center remote from the articular surface
- C. Geographic fat-equivalent marrow signal bounded by a sclerotic rim
- D. Diffuse homogeneous marrow edema with no discrete line and an intact cortex
Explanation: SIFK is identified by a subchondral low-signal fracture line paralleling the articular surface with surrounding marrow edema, whereas primary AVN shows the serpiginous double-line sign demarcating an epiphyseal infarct away from the subchondral plate. Line length/depth and overlying condylar collapse predict progression.

#### Advanced: marrow-3 · Bone marrow & fractures

A 31-year-old marathon runner has knee MRI showing patchy, slightly T1-hypointense marrow in the bilateral distal femoral and proximal tibial metadiaphyses, sparing the epiphyses. To confirm this is benign red-marrow reconversion rather than infiltrative disease, the MOST reliable feature is that the marrow signal remains:

- A. Higher (brighter) than adjacent skeletal muscle and intervertebral disc on T1-weighted images [CORRECT]
- B. Markedly lower than muscle on T1 but suppresses completely on STIR
- C. Isointense to fluid on T2 with avid homogeneous enhancement
- D. Lower than muscle on T1 with diffuse restricted diffusion
Explanation: Normal hematopoietic (red) marrow always retains interspersed fat, so on T1 it stays brighter than skeletal muscle and disc; signal at or below muscle should raise concern for marrow-replacing infiltration. Reconversion is also typically symmetric, spares the epiphyses/apophyses, and follows the reverse order of normal conversion.

#### Advanced: marrow-4 · Bone marrow & fractures

After an acute lateral patellar dislocation that has spontaneously reduced, which combination of marrow contusions on MRI is the characteristic signature of this mechanism?

- A. Inferomedial patellar facet and anterolateral femoral condyle (lateral aspect) [CORRECT]
- B. Superolateral patella and posteromedial tibial plateau
- C. Lateral patellar facet and lateral tibial plateau
- D. Medial patellar facet and medial femoral condyle
Explanation: As the patella dislocates laterally and relocates, the medial patellar facet impacts the anterolateral (lateral) femoral condyle, producing edema at both sites along with medial patellofemoral ligament (MPFL) injury (tear site variable — patellar, femoral, or both). Recognizing this bruise pattern lets you infer a transient dislocation even when the patella is reduced.

#### Advanced: technique-1 · MRI technique & artifacts

On a sagittal intermediate-TE PD sequence, the posterior horn of the lateral meniscus shows a focal increase in internal signal that does not extend to an articular surface. The signal is most conspicuous where the meniscus curves and the collagen fibers are oriented near 55 degrees to the main magnetic field. Which feature most reliably distinguishes this magic-angle phenomenon from a true intrasubstance degeneration/tear?

- A. The signal reaches an articular surface on at least one image
- B. The increased signal disappears or markedly decreases on a long-TE (T2-weighted) sequence [CORRECT]
- C. The signal is brightest on the fat-suppressed T2 image
- D. The signal is associated with a parameniscal cyst
Explanation: Magic-angle signal arises from T2 lengthening in collagen oriented near 55 degrees and is seen only at short/intermediate TE; it fades or vanishes at long TE, whereas a true tear/degeneration persists. This is why a meniscal signal that does not extend to the surface and resolves on T2 should not be called a tear.

#### Advanced: technique-2 · MRI technique & artifacts

A sagittal PD image of the knee shows a thin, sharply defined low-signal line paralleling the high-signal articular cartilage surface, mimicking a delamination or a surface fissure. The line is most pronounced at high-contrast interfaces and its position shifts if the phase- and frequency-encoding directions are swapped. What is the most effective way to reduce this truncation (Gibbs) artifact?

- A. Decrease the receiver bandwidth
- B. Increase the matrix size (more phase-encoding steps) to improve spatial resolution [CORRECT]
- C. Add fat suppression
- D. Increase the echo train length
Explanation: Truncation (Gibbs ringing) results from finite sampling of high-contrast edges and produces parallel lines that can mimic cartilage or meniscal abnormality; increasing the matrix/spatial resolution narrows the oscillations and reduces the artifact. It is most pronounced along the phase-encode direction and shifts when the encoding axes are swapped.

#### Advanced: technique-3 · MRI technique & artifacts

After ACL reconstruction with an interference screw, fluid-sensitive images near the tibial tunnel are degraded by susceptibility artifact obscuring the graft. Which single parameter change does the MOST to reduce metallic susceptibility distortion?

- A. Increasing the receiver bandwidth [CORRECT]
- B. Switching from fast spin-echo to gradient-echo acquisition
- C. Using spectral (chemical-shift selective) fat saturation instead of STIR
- D. Increasing the echo time (TE)
Explanation: Higher receiver bandwidth shortens the readout and compresses the per-pixel frequency shift, directly reducing metal-induced geometric distortion and signal pile-up. Gradient-echo worsens susceptibility, spectral fat-sat fails near metal (favoring STIR/Dixon), and longer TE increases dephasing.

#### Advanced: technique-4 · MRI technique & artifacts

On a coronal fat-suppressed T2 image of the knee, the marrow on the lateral side of the field appears bright and inhomogeneously 'unsuppressed,' while the medial side is well suppressed. There is no corresponding finding on T1. What is the most likely explanation for this fat-suppression failure?

- A. Bone marrow edema from a stress reaction
- B. B0 field inhomogeneity at the periphery causing the fat resonance to fall outside the saturation band [CORRECT]
- C. A true infiltrative marrow process
- D. Chemical-shift artifact of the second kind
Explanation: Spectral fat saturation depends on B0 homogeneity; toward the edges of the FOV (or near large off-isocenter curvature) the fat peak drifts and is incompletely saturated, producing regional 'failed' fat-sat that mimics marrow signal but has no T1 correlate. STIR or Dixon techniques, which are insensitive to B0 inhomogeneity, are the remedy.

#### Advanced: measurements-3 · Measurements & normal values

A 24-year-old gymnast has lateral patellar maltracking on physical exam. On a sagittal MRI you measure the patellar tendon length and the greatest diagonal length of the patella to calculate the Insall-Salvati ratio. Which value confirms patella alta?

- A. >1.2 [CORRECT]
- B. >0.8
- C. >1.0
- D. >2.0
Explanation: An Insall-Salvati ratio (patellar tendon length divided by patellar length) >1.2 indicates patella alta, a predisposing factor for instability; <0.8 indicates patella baja, and the normal range is approximately 0.8-1.2.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-1 · ACL tear — pivot-shift secondary signs

A 22-year-old has a non-contact pivoting injury. On this lateral sagittal slice through the intercondylar notch, identify the straight striated band that parallels Blumensaat's line. If that band were wavy, edematous, and discontinuous, which associated lateral injury should you most actively search for?

Plane: Sagittal PD-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-sagittal · startIndex: 21

- A. Posterolateral tibial plateau bone contusion [CORRECT]
- B. Anteromedial femoral condyle fracture
- C. Medial patellar facet bruise
- D. Quadriceps tendon avulsion
Explanation: A wavy, discontinuous ACL on this notch slice would indicate a tear, and the classic associated finding is the pivot-shift kissing contusion at the posterolateral tibial plateau and mid-lateral femoral condyle. A medial patellar facet bruise belongs to lateral patellar dislocation, a different mechanism, so it is not the expected ACL-associated finding.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-2 · PCL buckling — secondary sign of ACL tear

On this midline sagittal slice, find the thick curved low-signal band arcing through the posterior notch — the PCL. In a patient after a non-contact knee injury, if this intact band took on an exaggerated, buckled curve, what would that contour most likely indicate?

Plane: Sagittal PD-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-sagittal · startIndex: 16

- A. Anterior tibial translation from an ACL tear [CORRECT]
- B. An acute complete tear of this band
- C. Mucoid degeneration of this band
- D. A normal variant requiring no further evaluation
Explanation: This band is the PCL; an intact but abnormally buckled PCL arc is a recognized secondary sign of anterior tibial translation caused by an ACL tear, not a PCL injury itself. An acute PCL tear would instead show discontinuity or increased intrasubstance signal.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-3 · Meniscal extrusion threshold prompting root search

A 58-year-old felt a pop rising from a squat. On this mid-joint coronal slice, locate the medial meniscus body at the tibial margin. Measuring extrusion from the tibial cortex, beyond what amount at the mid-body should strongly prompt a posterior-root search?

Plane: Coronal PD-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-coronal · startIndex: 7

- A. 3 mm [CORRECT]
- B. 1 mm
- C. 8 mm
- D. Any visible extrusion
Explanation: Coronal medial meniscal extrusion of 3 mm or more beyond the tibial margin is a significant clue that should send you to the posterior root, especially with a compatible history. It is not diagnostic by itself: a 1 mm cutoff would overcall normal positioning, and requiring 8 mm would miss many clinically important root injuries.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-4 · MPFL tear — patellar dislocation bruise pattern

A 16-year-old presents after the kneecap briefly slipped out laterally and snapped back. On this axial patellofemoral slice, identify the medial patellar restraint (MPFL) along the medial patellar margin. If it were thickened with surrounding edema and fiber disruption, which paired bone-bruise pattern would most support the suspected mechanism?

Plane: Axial T2-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-axial · startIndex: 13

- A. Inferomedial patellar facet and anterolateral femoral condyle [CORRECT]
- B. Posterolateral tibial plateau and mid-lateral femoral condyle
- C. Medial femoral condyle and medial tibial plateau
- D. Lateral patellar facet and lateral tibial plateau
Explanation: The MPFL is the medial patellar restraint, and it tears during transient lateral patellar dislocation; the characteristic kissing contusions are at the inferomedial patellar facet and the anterolateral femoral condyle as the patella relocates. The posterolateral tibia/lateral femoral condyle pattern is the pivot-shift signature of an ACL tear, a different mechanism.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-5 · Trochlear dysplasia and patellar instability

A 24-year-old gymnast has recurrent lateral patellar instability. On this axial slice the patella tracks within the grooved anterior femur — the trochlea. If this groove were abnormally shallow and flat, which condition would that finding represent, and why does it matter here?

Plane: Axial T2-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-axial · startIndex: 13

- A. Trochlear dysplasia, a key predisposing factor for lateral patellar dislocation [CORRECT]
- B. Trochlear chondral defect causing mechanical locking
- C. Intercondylar notch stenosis predisposing to ACL tear
- D. Patella alta causing extensor lag
Explanation: The grooved anterior femur is the femoral trochlea; a shallow, flat groove with loss of the normal lateral-facet buttress is trochlear dysplasia, the principal bony risk factor for recurrent lateral patellar instability. Notch stenosis relates to ACL injury risk, not patellar tracking, so it does not explain this patient's lateral instability.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-6 · LCL / posterolateral corner — associated PCL injury

A 30-year-old had a varus hyperextension injury. On this mid-joint coronal slice, identify the cord-like lateral structure descending toward the fibular head — the LCL. If it were thickened and edematous with a small avulsion at the fibular styloid, which additional ligament would be most important to scrutinize for associated injury?

Plane: Coronal PD-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-coronal · startIndex: 7

- A. Posterior cruciate ligament [CORRECT]
- B. Medial collateral ligament
- C. Anterior cruciate ligament alone
- D. Transverse intermeniscal ligament
Explanation: This is the lateral (fibular) collateral ligament; injury here with a fibular styloid avulsion (arcuate sign) signals posterolateral corner disruption, which carries a high rate of associated PCL injury. The MCL is a medial-side structure on the opposite compartment and is not mechanically coupled to this lateral corner injury.

#### Image CAQ: icaq-7 · Lateral meniscus - normal pseudotears

On this sagittal compartment slice, identify the dark meniscal horns. A fellow sees a small cleft at the lateral meniscus near the popliteus tendon region and wants to call a tear. What should they do before making that diagnosis?

Plane: Sagittal PD-FS · Stack: /images/teaching/stacks/normal-knee-sagittal · startIndex: 8

- A. Trace the popliteal hiatus/popliteomeniscal fascicles and meniscofemoral/transverse ligaments across adjacent slices, then require surface-reaching tear signal or abnormal fascicle disruption [CORRECT]
- B. Call any cleft near the lateral meniscus a tear because normal ligaments do not contact the meniscus
- C. Ignore the finding entirely because lateral meniscal tears cannot occur at the posterior horn
- D. Measure medial meniscal extrusion; if it is under 3 mm, the lateral cleft is normal by definition
Explanation: The lateral meniscus has several normal relationships that create pseudotears: the anterior transverse intermeniscal ligament, the meniscofemoral ligaments near the posterior horn, and the popliteal hiatus/popliteomeniscal fascicles near the popliteus tendon. Trace them across slices and call a tear only when the signal behaves like a true surface-reaching tear or the fascicles/meniscocapsular support are disrupted.

#### Cross-plane: xp-meniscus

This is a meniscus on the sagittal — a low-signal “bow-tie.” Find the same meniscus on the coronal.

Same structure, different shape: the sagittal “bow-tie” becomes a triangular WEDGE pointing into the joint on the coronal. Flipping between the two views is how you build a 3-D model of each structure.

#### Cross-plane: xp-cruciate

This is the PCL in the intercondylar notch on the sagittal. On the coronal, find the central intercondylar region — the tibial spines that flank the notch.

The tibial spines (intercondylar eminence) mark the notch midline on the coronal. The cruciates run through the notch — PCL posterior, ACL anterior — not on the spines themselves; it's the same midline you track on the sagittal.

#### Cross-plane: xp-condyle

This is the LATERAL femoral condyle on the coronal (image-right). Find the lateral femoral condyle on the axial — mind the side.

Image-left is medial, image-right is lateral on BOTH planes. Behind the trochlea the two condyles flank the notch in cross-section — grab the lateral (right) one, not the medial.

#### Cross-plane: xp-plateau

This is the tibial plateau on the sagittal. Find the tibial plateau on the coronal.

The flat top of the tibia below the joint line. Coronal lets you compare the concave medial vs the convex lateral plateau side-by-side — the view for plateau fractures and depression.

#### Cross-plane: xp-cartilage

This is weight-bearing articular cartilage on the sagittal. Find the weight-bearing articular cartilage on the coronal.

Cartilage is the smooth intermediate-signal layer on the bone ends, outlined by a thin bright rim of fluid. On coronal you compare medial vs lateral cartilage thickness directly.

#### Cross-plane: xp-popliteal

These are the popliteal vessels behind the knee on the sagittal. Find them on the axial.

The popliteal artery and vein sit in the popliteal fossa posteriorly — the bottom of the axial image. A reliable posterior landmark on every plane.

#### Cross-plane: xp-patella

This is the patella on the axial. Find the patella on the sagittal.

On the sagittal, the patella is the anterior bone with the quadriceps tendon above and the patellar tendon below — the extensor mechanism. Anterior is to the LEFT.

#### Cross-plane: xp-medial-condyle

This is the MEDIAL femoral condyle on the coronal (image-left). Find the medial femoral condyle on the axial - mind the side.

Image-left is medial on BOTH planes. Behind the trochlea the two condyles flank the notch in cross-section - grab the medial (left) one, not the lateral. The mirror of the lateral drill; pick wrong and you mis-side a condylar lesion.

#### Cross-plane: xp-acl-origin

This is the ACL on the sagittal, attaching in the anterior intercondylar area. Find that region on the coronal - just in front of the central eminence.

The ACL is volume-averaged on coronal, so localize it to the anterior intercondylar area, in front of and between the tibial spines - not on a condyle. The PCL inserts far posteriorly, not here. Don't mistake normal spines for a tibial eminence avulsion and overcall.

#### Cross-plane: xp-patella-sag-axi

This is the PATELLA on the sagittal - a teardrop anterior to the femur. Find the same patella on the axial, where tracking is judged.

Rotate the sagittal teardrop 90 degrees and it becomes an inverted chevron, its median-ridge cartilage (thickest in the body) facing the trochlear groove. THE plane for tilt and lateral subluxation. Do not grab the trochlea - that is femur facing the patella.

#### Cross-plane: xp-marrow-t1

This is FEMORAL CONDYLE marrow on the PD-FS. Find the same condyle on the T1 - same slice, switched sequence.

Same shape, same plane - only the contrast flips. On T1 (no fat-sat) marrow fat is BRIGHT: your marrow and anatomy sequence. A discrete dark band = fracture; diffuse darkening = infiltration. Read marrow only on fat-suppressed and you overcall edema.

#### Cross-plane: xp-condyle-axi-cor

This is the LATERAL femoral condyle on the axial (image-right). Find the lateral femoral condyle on the coronal - mind the side.

Image-right is lateral on both planes. The rounded condyle cross-section on axial maps to the lateral of two side-by-side condyles on coronal - grab the right one. Coronal best assesses lateral compartment cartilage and tibiofemoral alignment.

#### Cross-plane: xp-plateau-cor-sag

This is the TIBIAL PLATEAU on the coronal - the top articular surface of the tibia. Find the same plateau on the sagittal, where you read it in profile.

Coronal shows the plateau head-on (concave medial, convex lateral); rotate to sagittal and you see it edge-on, in PROFILE with anterior and posterior slopes. That profile is the view for posterior-slope and depression-fracture assessment.

## Assessments

### Pre/Post Knowledge Items

#### kn-mri-basics-1-pre · parallel-pre · mri-basics

A 24-year-old recreational runner has medial knee pain after ramping up mileage. On the coronal T1-weighted image a thin curvilinear low-signal line parallels the medial femoral condyle subchondral plate, and you suspect an early subchondral stress (fatigue) fracture. Which additional sequence best confirms the surrounding reactive marrow edema?

- A. Non-fat-saturated T2, where the edema blends in against the adjacent bright marrow fat
- B. Fat-saturated PD/T2, where edema is fluid-bright against suppressed fat [CORRECT]
- C. Gradient-echo, which is the most sensitive sequence for marrow edema
- D. Non-fat-saturated T1, where the dark fracture line itself proves the edema
Explanation: Marrow edema is water-rich, so it is conspicuously fluid-bright on fluid-sensitive fat-saturated sequences (PD/T2 FS) where the normally bright fat signal is suppressed, maximizing contrast. Option D confuses the dark fracture line on T1 (which depicts the fracture, not the surrounding edema) with proof of edema, and option C reflects the common misconception that gradient-echo is sensitive for marrow edema when in fact susceptibility blooming makes it insensitive and unreliable here.

#### kn-mri-basics-1-post · parallel-post · mri-basics

A 30-year-old presents after a twisting injury. A focal area of the lateral femoral condyle marrow is dark on T1, and you must decide whether it represents acute trauma-related edema or simply fatty (yellow) marrow. Which cross-sequence signal behavior confirms it is edema rather than fat?

- A. It follows fat: bright on T1 and dark on fat-saturated PD/T2
- B. It stays dark on fat-saturated PD/T2, matching cortical bone
- C. It enhances only after IV gadolinium and is otherwise occult on all unenhanced sequences
- D. It is dark on T1 yet becomes fluid-bright (high signal) on fat-saturated PD/T2 [CORRECT]
Explanation: True marrow edema contains increased water, so it is low on T1 and turns fluid-bright on fat-saturated PD/T2 while normal marrow fat is suppressed; this cross-sequence behavior is what distinguishes edema from fat. Option A describes fatty marrow, which follows fat on every sequence and is not edema, and option C reflects an over-reliance-on-contrast misconception, since marrow edema is readily seen on unenhanced fluid-sensitive sequences and does not require gadolinium.

#### kn-normal-variants-1-pre · parallel-pre · normal-variants

On a sagittal PD image you see an oblique low-signal band coursing just anterior and inferior to the anterior horn of the lateral meniscus, creating an apparent cleft. Before calling an anterior horn tear, what is the most likely explanation?

- A. Transverse (intermeniscal) ligament producing a pseudotear at its junction with the meniscus [CORRECT]
- B. A radial tear of the lateral meniscus anterior horn
- C. Discoid meniscus with central degeneration
- D. Popliteus tendon within the popliteal hiatus
Explanation: The transverse intermeniscal ligament connects the anterior horns of the two menisci, and the fat-filled cleft at its attachment to the lateral anterior horn classically mimics a tear; tracing the band to the contralateral meniscus on adjacent slices confirms the variant. Option D (popliteus in the hiatus) mimics a POSTERIOR horn tear, not the anterior horn, and option B fails because a true radial tear would not be traceable as a continuous band crossing to the medial meniscus.

#### kn-normal-variants-1-post · parallel-post · normal-variants

On sagittal images a thin low-signal structure runs obliquely from the posterior horn of the lateral meniscus toward the medial femoral condyle, with a cleft at its meniscal origin that looks like a posterior horn tear. How do you confirm this is a normal variant?

- A. Trace it to its femoral attachment near the PCL, identifying a meniscofemoral ligament (Humphrey or Wrisberg) [CORRECT]
- B. Confirm grade-3 signal reaching the surface on two consecutive slices
- C. Note that the cleft disappears only on the coronal plane
- D. Identify the popliteus tendon passing through the popliteal hiatus
Explanation: The meniscofemoral ligaments of Humphrey (anterior to PCL) and Wrisberg (posterior to PCL) arise from the posterior horn of the lateral meniscus and insert on the medial femoral condyle; the fat cleft at their meniscal origin mimics a posterior horn tear, and tracing the band to its femoral attachment confirms the variant. Option B describes criteria for a true tear (the wrong conclusion here), and option D names a different lateral-meniscus pseudotear (popliteal hiatus) rather than the structure described.

#### kn-search-reporting-1-pre · parallel-pre · search-reporting

You confirm a complete ACL tear on a young pivoting athlete's MRI. Applying a systematic search, which structure must you deliberately re-inspect next because missing it would change surgical planning?

- A. The posteromedial meniscocapsular junction for a ramp lesion and the posterior meniscal roots [CORRECT]
- B. The quadriceps tendon insertion for tendinosis
- C. The prepatellar soft tissues for bursitis
- D. The proximal tibiofibular joint for a ganglion
Explanation: ACL tears are highly associated with ramp lesions at the posteromedial meniscocapsular junction and with posterior root tears, both of which are easily overlooked yet directly alter the surgeon's repair plan, so they belong in the directed post-ACL search and impression. Options C and D are real findings but are not management-changing companions of an ACL tear, and quadriceps tendinosis (option B) reflects satisfaction-of-search drift to an unrelated, low-yield structure.

#### kn-search-reporting-1-post · parallel-post · search-reporting

Your primary finding is a displaced bucket-handle tear of the medial meniscus with a fragment in the intercondylar notch. Following a disciplined search, what is the most important next structure to evaluate before finalizing the impression?

- A. The Hoffa fat pad for a focal ganglion cyst
- B. The iliotibial band for distal friction syndrome
- C. The ACL, because bucket-handle displacement is frequently associated with ACL insufficiency [CORRECT]
- D. The pes anserine bursa for fluid
Explanation: A displaced fragment in the notch can mimic or obscure the ACL, and bucket-handle tears are commonly associated with ACL injury, so the disciplined reader must verify ACL integrity (and avoid the 'double-PCL'/pseudo-intact ACL pitfall) before the impression. Options B and D are incidental, non-management-changing checks that represent drifting away from the high-yield, directed search prompted by the primary finding.

#### kn-marrow-mechanism-1-pre · parallel-pre · marrow-mechanism

A 19-year-old soccer player felt a pop while cutting. MRI shows kissing contusions at the lateral femoral condyle (sulcus terminalis region) and the posterolateral tibial plateau. Which ligament is most likely torn and should be scrutinized?

- A. Posterior cruciate ligament
- B. Anterior cruciate ligament [CORRECT]
- C. Medial patellofemoral ligament
- D. Deep medial collateral ligament
Explanation: The pivot-shift mechanism drives the lateral femoral condyle and posterolateral tibial plateau together, producing this kissing-contusion pattern that is the hallmark of ACL rupture. The PCL (option A) is associated with an anterior proximal tibia contusion from a dashboard blow, and the MPFL (option C) pairs with a medial patella plus anterolateral femoral condyle pattern of transient patellar dislocation, both distinct mechanisms.

#### kn-marrow-mechanism-1-post · parallel-post · marrow-mechanism

A teenager dislocated the patella while pivoting, then it spontaneously reduced. Which combination of marrow edema and the associated structure to evaluate fits this mechanism?

- A. Anterior proximal tibia edema; evaluate the PCL
- B. Lateral femoral condyle and posterolateral plateau edema; evaluate the ACL
- C. Medial patellar facet and anterolateral femoral condyle edema; evaluate the medial patellofemoral ligament [CORRECT]
- D. Medial femoral condyle subchondral edema; evaluate the deep MCL
Explanation: Transient lateral patellar dislocation produces a characteristic bone-bruise pair on the medial patellar facet (from MPFL avulsion impaction) and the anterolateral femoral condyle (from contact during relocation), so the MPFL must be assessed along with the search for osteochondral fragments. Option B is the pivot-shift/ACL pattern and option A is the dashboard/PCL pattern, both representing different injury mechanisms that trainees often conflate with patellar dislocation.

#### kn-marrow-mechanism-2-pre · parallel-pre · marrow-mechanism

A 68-year-old woman has acute medial knee pain with no significant trauma. MRI shows a focal subchondral low-signal line paralleling the medial femoral condyle articular surface with surrounding marrow edema. Which feature best distinguishes a subchondral insufficiency fracture (SIFK) from a simple traumatic contusion?

- A. Diffuse, ill-defined marrow edema with no discrete subchondral line and an antecedent impact
- B. A discrete subchondral low-signal fracture line subjacent to the articular surface with adjacent edema, often without notable trauma [CORRECT]
- C. Edema confined to the patella after a dashboard injury
- D. Bright T1 fatty signal replacing the subchondral bone
Explanation: SIFK is defined by a discrete subchondral fracture line (low signal on all sequences) hugging the articular surface with surrounding edema, typically in older or osteoporotic patients without significant trauma. A traumatic contusion (option A) shows geographic, ill-defined edema WITHOUT a subchondral line and follows a known impact; option D describes benign fatty marrow, not an acute insufficiency fracture, a distinction trainees frequently miss.

#### kn-marrow-mechanism-2-post · parallel-post · marrow-mechanism

A 70-year-old man develops sudden medial knee pain. MRI shows extensive medial femoral condyle marrow edema, a subchondral focus, and overlying articular surface flattening. Which finding most supports subchondral insufficiency fracture (formerly 'spontaneous osteonecrosis,' SONK) rather than true avascular osteonecrosis?

- A. A linear subchondral fracture line with adjacent edema and articular surface collapse, in an osteoporotic patient without steroid or alcohol history [CORRECT]
- B. A serpentine double-line sign demarcating a wedge-shaped epiphyseal infarct
- C. Diffuse patchy marrow signal sparing the subchondral region
- D. Bilateral symmetric metaphyseal infarcts
Explanation: Current teaching reframes 'SONK' as a subchondral insufficiency fracture: a linear subchondral fracture with adjacent edema and possible surface collapse in an older/osteoporotic patient, without the classic AVN risk factors. The serpentine double-line sign and discrete wedge-shaped or metaphyseal infarcts (options B and D) characterize true avascular necrosis, which trainees often incorrectly equate with this insufficiency-fracture presentation.

#### kn-menisci-1-pre · parallel-pre · menisci

A 58-year-old presents after a deep squat with acute medial knee pain. Coronal PD fat-sat MRI shows a radial-oriented avulsion at the posterior root insertion of the medial meniscus, and on the coronal image the meniscal body is displaced 4 mm beyond the tibial margin. Functionally, how should this finding be characterized in your report?

- A. A stable degenerative intrasubstance change that requires no specific comment
- B. A peripheral longitudinal tear that is likely to heal given the vascular zone
- C. Loss of meniscal hoop-stress function, biomechanically equivalent to a near-total meniscectomy [CORRECT]
- D. An isolated meniscocapsular separation without effect on load distribution
Explanation: A posterior root avulsion uncouples the circumferential collagen ring, so the meniscus can no longer convert axial load into hoop (circumferential) stress; the joint then behaves as if the meniscus were absent, producing rapid compartment overload — hence the report must flag it as functionally a meniscectomy. Option B reflects the common error of treating a root avulsion like a healable peripheral longitudinal tear; the root is an avulsion of the anchor, not a vertical tear in vascular tissue. Option A misclassifies a mechanically catastrophic lesion as benign degeneration, and D confuses a meniscocapsular (ramp-type) injury with the root, which has very different load consequences.

#### kn-menisci-1-post · parallel-post · menisci

A 61-year-old reports a pop while gardening. Sagittal images show fluid signal cleaving the posterior horn of the medial meniscus at its tibial insertion, and on the coronal image the body extrudes 5 mm medial to the tibial plateau edge. How does this lesion alter the biomechanics of the involved compartment?

- A. It mildly stiffens the meniscus but preserves overall load transmission
- B. It eliminates effective hoop-stress transmission, transferring load onto the articular cartilage as in a meniscectomized knee [CORRECT]
- C. It only affects rotational stability, leaving axial load distribution intact
- D. It produces a self-limited synovitis without measurable load redistribution
Explanation: Detachment of the posterior root releases the circumferential fibers so axial load is no longer converted into hoop stress and is dumped onto the chondral surface. In cadaveric testing, a medial posterior root tear raised medial-compartment peak contact pressure by 25% with no detectable difference from total medial meniscectomy, and root repair restored pressures to normal (Allaire 2008) — which is why the finding is reported as functionally equivalent to a meniscectomy and why repair, not observation, is the surgical goal. Option A understates the lesion by treating a complete root avulsion as a partial stiffening change. Options C and D reflect misconceptions that the root governs only rotation or is merely an inflammatory finding, when in fact it is the principal anchor for compartment load-bearing.

#### kn-menisci-2-pre · parallel-pre · menisci

A 47-year-old has lateral joint-line pain. On sequential sagittal PD fat-sat images the lateral meniscus shows a segment where the normal triangular meniscal tissue abruptly vanishes, leaving a 'ghost' of absent meniscus, and the coronal image shows a vertical cleft splitting the meniscus perpendicular to its free edge. Which tear morphology does this pattern indicate?

- A. A radial tear, with the 'cleft/ghost meniscus' sign reflecting a tear perpendicular to the circumferential fibers [CORRECT]
- B. A peripheral longitudinal tear paralleling the meniscal circumference
- C. An intrasubstance (grade-2) degeneration not reaching the surface
- D. A meniscocapsular (ramp) separation at the posteromedial corner
Explanation: A radial tear runs perpendicular to the long axis of the meniscus, cutting across the circumferential collagen bundles; on the plane where the tear is in-plane the meniscus appears truncated or absent (the 'ghost meniscus' sign), with a vertical cleft on the orthogonal plane. Option B describes a longitudinal tear, which parallels rather than crosses the circumferential fibers and does not produce a ghost segment, and option C is intrasubstance signal that does not reach a surface, so it cannot produce a through-and-through cleft. Option D is a peripheral capsular separation on the medial side, a different location and morphology entirely.

#### kn-menisci-2-post · parallel-post · menisci

On a knee MRI, the medial meniscus body shows fluid-bright signal oriented perpendicular to its free margin on the coronal image, and on the adjacent sagittal slice the meniscus body looks shortened with a missing segment. The remaining meniscus is otherwise normal in position. How is this lesion best classified, and what is its key biomechanical consequence?

- A. A peripheral longitudinal tear; it usually preserves hoop-stress function
- B. A parameniscal cyst; it indicates a horizontal-cleavage tear with fluid tracking
- C. A bucket-handle tear; the fragment has displaced into the notch
- D. A radial tear extending to the free edge; it disrupts circumferential fibers and compromises hoop-stress distribution [CORRECT]
Explanation: A cleft perpendicular to the free edge with a truncated/absent meniscal segment on the orthogonal plane is the signature of a radial tear; because it transects the circumferential collagen bundles, a full-thickness radial tear disrupts hoop-stress generation much like a root tear and predisposes to extrusion and chondral overload. Option A mislabels the perpendicular cleft as a longitudinal tear, which parallels the fibers and does preserve hoop stress; option C invents a displaced bucket-handle fragment in the notch that is not described here. Option B mistakes a through-substance radial cleft for a parameniscal cyst, which would be a rounded T2-bright collection adjacent to a horizontal tear.

#### kn-menisci-3-pre · parallel-pre · menisci

A 19-year-old soccer player has a locked knee. Sagittal MRI shows a second low-signal band lying anterior and parallel to the posterior cruciate ligament within the intercondylar notch. The medial meniscus appears truncated. Which diagnosis does this fragment configuration indicate?

- A. A bucket-handle meniscal tear with the fragment displaced into the intercondylar notch [CORRECT]
- B. A mucoid-degenerated PCL producing a thickened ligament
- C. A meniscal root tear with posterior extrusion of the meniscal body
- D. A torn meniscofemoral ligament of Wrisberg mimicking a second ligament
Explanation: A fragment lying parallel and anterior to the PCL — the 'double-PCL sign' — is the hallmark of a displaced bucket-handle tear, with the inner meniscal fragment flipped into the notch and a correspondingly truncated parent meniscus. Option B reflects the misconception that a doubled appearance equals PCL pathology, but mucoid degeneration thickens the PCL itself rather than adding a separate band. Option C describes extrusion (peripheral, not into the notch), and D confuses a normal meniscofemoral ligament with a displaced fragment; the Wrisberg ligament runs posterior to the PCL and is much thinner than a bucket-handle fragment.

#### kn-menisci-3-post · parallel-post · menisci

Reviewing a knee MRI, you see grade-3 linear signal reaching the inferior articular surface of the medial meniscus posterior horn on two consecutive sagittal images, and the same defect is again visible reaching the surface on the coronal sequence. How should this be reported under the two-slice-touch criterion?

- A. Indeterminate, because surface signal must be confirmed on three or more contiguous slices
- B. A normal magic-angle artifact, since linear signal can be artifactual at the posterior horn
- C. A definite meniscal tear, since surface-reaching signal is present on two images and corroborated on a second plane [CORRECT]
- D. An intrasubstance grade-2 signal that does not warrant a tear diagnosis
Explanation: The two-slice-touch rule diagnoses a tear when grade-3 signal contacts a meniscal surface on at least two images, and cross-plane confirmation (here on the coronal) further increases confidence — so this is a definite tear. Option A overstates the threshold by demanding three slices, and option D mislabels surface-reaching grade-3 signal as intrasubstance grade-2, which by definition does not reach the surface. Option B invokes magic-angle artifact, which occurs at ~55 degrees to B0 on short-TE sequences and does not reach an articular surface on fluid-sensitive images across two planes.

#### kn-cartilage-osteochondral-1-pre · parallel-pre · cartilage-osteochondral

A 34-year-old has a well-corticated osteochondral lesion of the lateral femoral condyle. On the fat-sat T2 image, a continuous line of fluid-equivalent high signal undercuts the entire fragment at its interface with the parent bone. Which conclusion about stability is best supported?

- A. The lesion is stable because the fragment retains its corticated margin
- B. The lesion is unstable, as a fluid-signal rim completely separating the fragment indicates loss of osseous bridging [CORRECT]
- C. Stability cannot be assessed without intravenous contrast in every case
- D. The fluid line represents normal red-marrow signal and is reassuring
Explanation: A T2 fluid-equivalent rim that completely encircles the fragment interface signifies fluid tracking between fragment and donor site (loss of osseous continuity) and is a primary MRI sign of an unstable osteochondral lesion, alongside subchondral cysts, an overlying cartilage breach, and a displaced fragment. Option A is the classic trap that a corticated margin equals stability, whereas a complete high-signal rim outweighs that reassurance. Option D confuses bright fluid at the interface with normal hematopoietic marrow (which is intermediate, not fluid-bright), and C overstates the role of contrast, which is adjunctive rather than mandatory.

#### kn-cartilage-osteochondral-1-post · parallel-post · cartilage-osteochondral

A 13-year-old gymnast with open physes has a medial femoral condyle osteochondral lesion showing a thin band of high T2 signal at the fragment interface. There is no subchondral cyst, the overlying cartilage is intact, and the fragment is in situ. How should you interpret the interface signal in this patient?

- A. The interface signal indicates an unstable lesion warranting surgical fixation
- B. The lesion is stable because skeletally immature patients heal reliably
- C. Interface hyperintensity is less specific for instability in juveniles, so combine it with other signs and clinical findings before calling it unstable [CORRECT]
- D. Open physes make MRI uninterpretable for osteochondral lesions
Explanation: In juvenile osteochondritis dissecans with open physes, T2 interface hyperintensity can reflect vascular granulation tissue in a healing, still-stable lesion, so it is less specific than in adults; the call requires corroborating signs (a surrounding cyst, encircling fluid rim, cartilage breach, or displacement) plus clinical correlation. Option A applies the adult fluid-rim rule too rigidly to a child, the most common pediatric pitfall, while option B errs the opposite way by assuming immaturity guarantees stability. Option D is incorrect because MRI remains the primary tool — it simply must be interpreted with the juvenile caveat in mind.

#### kn-cartilage-osteochondral-2-pre · parallel-pre · cartilage-osteochondral

On a fat-suppressed PD image of the trochlea, you see a focal fluid-bright defect in the articular cartilage that extends through the full cartilage thickness down to, but not into, the subchondral bone plate. How should this chondral lesion be graded and reported?

- A. A superficial partial-thickness defect involving less than 50% of cartilage depth
- B. Subchondral marrow edema only, with the cartilage intact
- C. An osteochondral fracture, because the subchondral bone is involved
- D. A full-thickness chondral defect reaching the subchondral plate without bony involvement [CORRECT]
Explanation: Fluid signal filling a defect that spans the entire cartilage depth down to the subchondral bone, with the bone plate intact, is a full-thickness chondral defect. Watch the grading system, because the two label this differently: it is modified Outerbridge grade IV (full-thickness loss with the plate exposed), but ICRS grade 3 — specifically ICRS 3C, 'down to but not through the subchondral bone' — because ICRS reserves grade 4 for a defect that breaches the subchondral plate into bone. Either way it remains a chondral, not osteochondral, injury. Option A undercalls a full-thickness defect as a partial one (<50% depth). Option C overcalls it as an osteochondral fracture; the subchondral bone is preserved here, distinguishing a pure chondral defect from an osteochondral one. Option B ignores the visible cartilage defect entirely.

#### kn-cartilage-osteochondral-2-post · parallel-post · cartilage-osteochondral

A 27-year-old runner has knee pain. Fat-sat PD images show a focal cartilage irregularity at the medial femoral condyle where joint fluid undercuts a flap of cartilage, but the deepest fibers of cartilage and the underlying bone remain in contact with the plate. Which grade best describes this lesion?

- A. A partial-thickness chondral defect (cartilage flap/fissure) not extending to the subchondral bone [CORRECT]
- B. A full-thickness defect with exposed subchondral bone
- C. An unstable osteochondral lesion with a displaced osseous fragment
- D. Normal cartilage with magic-angle artifact simulating a defect
Explanation: Fluid undercutting a cartilage flap while the deep cartilage fibers still abut the subchondral plate is a partial-thickness defect (a fissure or delamination flap), graded below full-thickness because the lesion does not reach bare bone. Option B overcalls the depth — there is no exposed subchondral bone — and option C invents an osseous fragment that is not present, confusing a chondral flap with an osteochondral lesion. Option D dismisses a genuine fluid-filled defect as magic-angle artifact, which would not show joint fluid tracking under a flap on fluid-sensitive sequences.

#### kn-cruciates-1-pre · parallel-pre · cruciates

A 22-year-old soccer player has an acute non-contact pivoting injury. On the sagittal proton-density fat-saturated images, the radiologist wants to confirm a complete ACL tear using the most specific (primary) imaging criterion. Which finding most directly establishes the diagnosis?

- A. A curvilinear cortical avulsion at the lateral proximal tibia (Segond fragment)
- B. Anterior translation of the tibia relative to the femur of more than 7 mm
- C. Bone-marrow edema in the lateral femoral condyle and posterolateral tibial plateau
- D. Full-thickness discontinuity of the ACL fibers with intervening high T2 signal in the expected ligament course [CORRECT]
Explanation: The direct/primary sign of a complete ACL tear is frank fiber discontinuity with abnormal T2 signal filling the expected ligament course; this is the most specific criterion. Anterior tibial translation (B) and pivot-shift kissing contusions in the lateral femoral condyle/posterolateral tibia (C) are secondary signs that support but do not by themselves prove a tear. A Segond fragment (A) is a lateral capsular avulsion strongly associated with ACL injury but is itself only an indirect marker, not the primary ligamentous sign.

#### kn-cruciates-1-post · parallel-post · cruciates

A 30-year-old basketball player landed awkwardly and felt a pop. On MRI the radiologist must decide which single finding constitutes the direct (primary) evidence of a complete ACL tear rather than a supportive secondary sign. Which observation provides that direct evidence?

- A. A deep lateral femoral notch (terminal sulcus) deeper than 1.5 mm
- B. Buckling of the PCL into an acute angulated (wavy) configuration
- C. Uncovering of the posterior horn of the lateral meniscus by the tibia
- D. Complete absence of intact ACL fibers spanning the notch with edematous signal replacing the ligament [CORRECT]
Explanation: Direct visualization of absent/discontinuous ACL fibers replaced by edematous signal across the notch is the primary sign that defines a complete tear. A deep lateral femoral notch (A), a buckled PCL (B), and posterior uncovering of the lateral meniscus (C) are all secondary signs reflecting anterior tibial translation; trainees often over-rely on these, but they only suggest ACL incompetence and can occur with chronic laxity, so they cannot independently establish an acute complete tear.

#### kn-cruciates-2-pre · parallel-pre · cruciates

A 48-year-old runner has chronic vague posterior knee pain with no instability on exam. MRI shows a thickened ACL with intermediate T2 signal interspersed between intact, continuous, well-tensioned fibers, giving a striated appearance, and there is no anterior tibial translation. What is the most likely diagnosis?

- A. Acute complete ACL tear
- B. Chronic partial-thickness ACL tear with fiber retraction
- C. Mucoid degeneration of the ACL [CORRECT]
- D. ACL ganglion cyst causing fiber rupture
Explanation: Mucoid degeneration produces a thickened, T2-hyperintense ACL with preserved, continuous, taut fibers — the classic 'celery stalk' appearance — and no secondary signs of instability, exactly as described. An acute complete tear (A) shows fiber discontinuity and translation, which are absent here. A partial tear with retraction (B) would show some disrupted fibers; the hallmark of mucoid degeneration is that the fibers remain intact, which is the point trainees most often miss when they overcall a tear. A ganglion (D) is a discrete cyst, not diffuse intrasubstance striation, and does not by itself rupture fibers.

#### kn-cruciates-2-post · parallel-post · cruciates

A 53-year-old golfer reports an aching knee with a sense of fullness on deep flexion but a stable Lachman test. MRI demonstrates an enlarged ACL of increased T2 signal in which the individual fascicles remain continuous and longitudinally oriented, with no bone bruise and a normal tibiofemoral relationship. Which interpretation is correct?

- A. Complete mid-substance ACL tear with the empty-notch sign
- B. Mucoid degeneration of the ACL, not a tear [CORRECT]
- C. High-grade partial ACL tear involving the anteromedial bundle
- D. Acute interstitial ACL sprain with intrasubstance hemorrhage
Explanation: Continuous, longitudinally oriented fibers with diffuse T2 signal (the 'celery stalk') and absent secondary signs in a clinically stable knee indicate mucoid degeneration rather than a tear. A complete tear (A) requires fiber discontinuity and an empty notch, which are absent. A high-grade partial tear (C) would disrupt some fascicles and often correlate with laxity; preserved continuity argues against it. An acute interstitial sprain (D) typically accompanies trauma and bone-marrow edema, which this chronic, atraumatic, bruise-free case lacks — the common error is mistaking benign mucoid signal for a tear.

#### kn-collateral-corner-1-pre · parallel-pre · collateral-corner

A 25-year-old skier has an acute valgus-and-rotation injury. MRI shows a curvilinear bony fragment avulsed from the lateral margin of the proximal tibia just below the joint line. Recognizing this finding should most prompt the radiologist to scrutinize which structure?

- A. The medial patellofemoral ligament
- B. The quadriceps tendon insertion
- C. The pes anserinus tendons
- D. The anterior cruciate ligament [CORRECT]
Explanation: The described fragment is a Segond fracture, an avulsion of the lateral capsular/anterolateral ligament complex that carries a very high association with ACL tears (and often meniscal injury); seeing it mandates a careful look at the ACL. The quadriceps tendon (B) and pes anserinus (C) are unrelated to this lateral capsular avulsion. The MPFL (A) is implicated in patellar dislocation, a different mechanism; trainees sometimes confuse the lateral Segond avulsion with patellofemoral injuries, but the Segond's key teaching point is its ACL association.

#### kn-collateral-corner-1-post · parallel-post · collateral-corner

A 28-year-old footballer sustained a pivoting injury. Coronal MRI reveals a thin vertically oriented cortical avulsion fragment along the lateral aspect of the proximal tibia at the site of the anterolateral capsular attachment. Identifying this fragment should raise the highest suspicion for concurrent injury of which structure?

- A. The anterior cruciate ligament [CORRECT]
- B. The posterior cruciate ligament
- C. The patellar tendon
- D. The iliotibial band proximal origin
Explanation: This is a Segond fracture — a lateral capsular avulsion — and its principal clinical significance is the strong association with an ACL tear (plus frequent meniscal tears), so the ACL is the priority for further evaluation. A PCL avulsion (B) is a posterior/medial mechanism, not this lateral fragment. The patellar tendon (C) attaches at the tibial tubercle anteriorly, not the lateral capsular margin. The ITB (D) is sometimes invoked because of its lateral location, but the avulsed structure here is the anterolateral capsule/ligament, whose recognized companion injury is the ACL.

#### kn-extensor-synovium-1-pre · parallel-pre · extensor-synovium

A 58-year-old man could not extend his knee after stumbling. Sagittal MRI shows a full-thickness gap with fluid signal in the tendon immediately superior to the superior pole of the patella, and the patella sits abnormally low. Which diagnosis best fits these findings?

- A. Quadriceps tendon rupture with patella baja [CORRECT]
- B. Patellar tendon rupture with patella alta
- C. Quadriceps tendinosis without a tear
- D. Patellar tendon rupture with patella baja
Explanation: A defect just above the superior patellar pole localizes to the quadriceps tendon; loss of the proximal extensor restraint lets the patellar tendon pull the patella inferiorly, producing patella baja. Patellar tendon rupture (B, D) occurs below the inferior pole and produces patella alta because the unopposed quadriceps retracts the patella superiorly — so 'patellar tendon with baja' (D) is internally inconsistent. Tendinosis (C) shows thickening and intermediate signal without a full-thickness fluid-filled gap and would not cause inability to extend; mixing up the tendon involved and the resulting patellar position is the classic trainee error.

#### kn-extensor-synovium-1-post · parallel-post · extensor-synovium

A 34-year-old basketball player felt a pop while jumping and cannot actively straighten the knee. Sagittal MRI shows a full-thickness fluid-filled gap in the tendon just distal to the inferior pole of the patella, and the patella is positioned abnormally high. Which diagnosis best accounts for these findings?

- A. Quadriceps tendon rupture with patella alta
- B. Patellar tendinosis without a discrete tear
- C. Patellar tendon rupture with patella alta [CORRECT]
- D. Quadriceps tendon rupture with patella baja
Explanation: A full-thickness gap just below the inferior patellar pole is a patellar tendon rupture; with the distal tether lost, the intact quadriceps retracts the patella upward, yielding patella alta. A quadriceps rupture (A, D) involves the tendon above the superior pole and produces patella baja, so 'quadriceps with alta' (A) is internally inconsistent. Patellar tendinosis (B) shows fusiform thickening with intermediate signal and intact fibers, not a fluid-filled discontinuity, and preserves active extension; confusing which tendon failed and the direction the patella migrates is the high-yield distinction being tested.

### Confidence Statements

- **kc-mri-basics (mri-basics):** I can select the appropriate MRI sequence for a given knee question and interpret fluid-sensitive fat-saturated signal to distinguish true pathology from normal tissue.
- **kc-normal-variants (normal-variants):** I can recognize normal knee variants such as the popliteal hiatus and meniscofemoral ligaments and tell them apart from a genuine meniscal or ligament tear.
- **kc-search-reporting (search-reporting):** I can apply a systematic knee MRI search pattern that avoids satisfaction of search and produce a structured impression that prioritizes management-changing findings.
- **kc-marrow-mechanism (marrow-mechanism):** I can read bone marrow edema patterns to infer the injury mechanism (pivot-shift, dashboard/PCL, patellar dislocation) and distinguish insufficiency edema from traumatic contusion.
- **kc-menisci (menisci):** I can systematically trace each meniscus and its roots to identify root tears, ramp lesions, and bucket-handle tears, measure extrusion, and apply the two-slice-touch rule.
- **kc-cartilage-osteochondral (cartilage-osteochondral):** I can grade chondral lesions and judge osteochondral-fragment stability using interface fluid signal, cysts, and cartilage breach, accounting for reduced specificity in juveniles.
- **kc-cruciates (cruciates):** I can diagnose ACL and PCL tears from direct fiber-discontinuity signs and distinguish a true tear from mucoid degeneration with preserved fiber continuity.
- **kc-collateral-corner (collateral-corner):** I can evaluate the collateral ligaments and the posterolateral and posteromedial corners as combined injury patterns rather than isolated findings.
- **kc-extensor-synovium (extensor-synovium):** I can assess the quadriceps and patellar tendons, characterize a joint effusion versus a Baker cyst, and identify loose bodies in the synovial compartments.

## Red Flags

- **Bicruciate injury / knee dislocation:** Even if spontaneously reduced — clear the popliteal artery (flow voids) and common peroneal nerve. A missed vascular injury threatens the limb. Urgent.
- **Locked knee + displaced bucket-handle tear:** Double-PCL sign or fewer than two meniscal bow-ties = a fragment in the notch blocking extension — mechanical, needs surgery, not a brace.
- **Extensor mechanism rupture (quadriceps / patellar tendon):** Full-thickness gap + can't extend = surgical repair. Don't undercall a high-grade partial tear.
- **Marrow-replacing lesion:** Low-T1 marrow that isn't a contusion — tumor or infiltration until proven otherwise. Don't write it off as edema.
- **Segond / reverse Segond fracture:** A lateral tibial rim avulsion points to an ACL tear (~75–100%); a posteromedial avulsion points to the PCL. The fracture flags the ligament — go look.

## Quick Reference

## Daily Pearls

- **1:** The pivot-shift contusion pattern (posterolateral tibial plateau + lateral femoral condyle) is highly suggestive of ACL injury; confirm the ACL directly and search for associated meniscal tears.
- **2:** Meniscal signal that unequivocally reaches an articular surface is suspicious for a tear. Surface contact on two consecutive standard slices greatly increases confidence, but a one-slice finding still requires morphology and orthogonal-plane correlation rather than automatic dismissal.
- **3:** In every ACL tear, actively search for a ramp lesion at the posterior meniscocapsular junction — they’re missed on up to 50% of preoperative MRIs.
- **4:** Meniscal extrusion >3 mm is abnormal and should trigger a careful search for root or radial tears and degeneration; extrusion alone does not diagnose a root tear. Trace the root on coronal and axial images.
- **5:** The ‘absent bow-tie sign’ (fewer than two consecutive sagittal body segments) indicates a bucket-handle tear with fragment displacement.
- **6:** The MPFL most commonly tears at its femoral insertion (~50% of cases), though mid-substance and patellar-sided tears also occur. Check the adductor tubercle region on axial images.
